            interstitial: googletag.sizeMapping()
                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()

        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adma.202410857')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '6f1461ff-6730-40e0-8e29-b5f11ab42b37'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d132527944" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with low cost and environmentally friendly features have recently attracted significant attention for renewable energy storage. Sodium layer oxides stand out as a type of promising cathode material for SIBs owing to their high capacity, good rate performance, and high compatibility for manufacturing. However, the poor cycling stability of layer oxide cathodes due to structure distortion greatly impacts their practical applications. Herein, a high entropy doped Cu, Fe, and Mn-based layered oxide (HE-CFMO), Na<sub>0.95</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.20</sub>Fe<sub>0.22</sub>Mn<sub>0.35</sub>Ti<sub>0.13</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for high-performance SIBs, is designed. The HE-CFMO cathode possesses high-entropy transition metal (TM) layers with a homogeneous stress distribution, providing a moderated interlayer spacing to maintain the structure stability and enhance Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion. In addition, Li doping in TM layers increases the Mn valence state, which effectively suppresses John–Teller effect, thus stabilizing the layered structure during cycling. Furthermore, the use of nontoxic and low-cost raw materials benefits future commercialization and reduces the risk of environmental pollution. As a result, the HE-CFMO cathode exhibits a super cycling performance with a 95% capacity retention after 300 cycles. This work provides a promising strategy to improve the structure stability and reaction kinetics of cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410857-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410857-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), developed side-by-side with lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), exhibit great potential for large-scale renewable energy storage systems due to their low cost, wide working conditions, higher safety, and abundant sources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0001_R_d132527935e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - J. Solid State Chem.">1</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0005_R_d132527935e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Many cathode materials have been investigated to accommodate Na ion insertion/extraction for alleviating the structural distortion, such as Prussian blue analogs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0006_R_d132527935e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Stor. Mater.">6</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0009_R_d132527935e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small Structures">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanionic materials (including phosphates, fluorophosphate, and other mixed phosphates),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0010_R_d132527935e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Power Sources">10</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0015_R_d132527935e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and layered oxide materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0016_R_d132527935e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0019_R_d132527935e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Energy Chem.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these materials, sodium-layered oxide materials (SLOs) with a formula of Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (TM = transition metals) are considered to be the most promising cathode material considering their excellent structural compatibility for Na ion insertion/extraction, relatively high energy density, low cost, and ease of manufacturing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0003_R_d132527935e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Mater. Res. Bull.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0020_R_d132527935e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0028_R_d132527935e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Power Sources">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, Mn-based SLOs without toxic Co and Ni components are more competitive because of their economic and environmental sustainability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0029_R_d132527935e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Solid State Chem.">29</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0031_R_d132527935e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, Mn-based SLOs show relatively low specific capacity, sluggish ion diffusion kinetics, and poor cycling stability caused by the unstable layered structure due to John–Teller effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0032_R_d132527935e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Several strategies have been employed to improve the electrochemical performance of SLO cathode materials, such as surface modification,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0033_R_d132527935e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0035_R_d132527935e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Solid State Ionics">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> morphology design,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0036_R_d132527935e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Commun.">36</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0037_R_d132527935e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> heteroatom doping, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0037_R_d132527935e620" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">37</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0042_R_d132527935e620" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, heteroatom doping is the most promising strategy to enhance the structure stability and electrochemical performance of SLOs. Heteroatom doping strategies can be generally classified as single-atom doping, dual-atom doping, and multiple-atom doping strategies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0032_R_d132527935e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The single-atom doping strategy only introduces one type of element into the TM layers of SLOs, such as doping Mg or Cu to improve Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0038_R_d132527935e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - RSC Adv.">38</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0039_R_d132527935e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Energy Chem.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> doping Ti to relieve the structure distortion,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0040_R_d132527935e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and doping Sn to inhibit the structure collapse at “high” voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0041_R_d132527935e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Energy Lett.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The dual-atom doping strategies apply two types of dopants to improve performance, such as co-doping Cu and Ti to improve the air-exposure and cycling stability,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0037_R_d132527935e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and co-doping Ni and Ti to increase the discharge voltage and stabilize crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0042_R_d132527935e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The multiple-atom doping strategies use three or more types of dopants to optimize SLOs, such as the high-entropy doping strategy, which has been introduced to SLO design recently.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0043_R_d132527935e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Environ. Sci.">43</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0046_R_d132527935e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-entropy doping design requires the configurational entropy of TM layers to be ≥ 1.5R (R represents the universal gas constant) after doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0047_R_d132527935e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Scripta Materialia">47</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0048_R_d132527935e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Frontiers in Energy Research">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With high entropy doping design, SLOs, such as NaNi<sub>0.12</sub>Cu<sub>0.12</sub>Mg<sub>0.12</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>Sb<sub>0.04</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0049_R_d132527935e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>0.95</sub>Li<sub>0.06</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.49</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0044_R_d132527935e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nanomicro Lett.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>0.9</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.15</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0050_R_d132527935e736" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>0.7</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>1.95</sub>F<sub>0.1</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0051_R_d132527935e758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Stor. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>0.95</sub>Li<sub>0.07</sub>Cu<sub>0.11</sub>Ni<sub>0.11</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.41</sub>O<sub>1.97</sub>F<sub>0.03</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0052_R_d132527935e781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na(Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>)O<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0053_R_d132527935e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Mater. Chem. A">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na(Fe<sub>0.2</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Cu<sub>0.05</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>)B<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0054_R_d132527935e823" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Energy Chem.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> can effectively maintain a stable layered structure and provide improved electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0046_R_d132527935e829" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0055_R_d132527935e829" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Energy Stor. Mater.">55</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0057_R_d132527935e829" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Small Methods">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these high entropy-doped SLOs usually contain undesirable Co and Ni contents.</p>
         
         <p>On the other hand, optimizing interlayer distances could also improve Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in the bulk phase of SLOs. Many studies indicated that large interlayer spacing between adjacent transition metal (TM) layers in high entropy designs can be beneficial to Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0019_R_d132527935e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Energy Chem.">19</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0049_R_d132527935e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">49</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0051_R_d132527935e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Stor. Mater.">51</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0056_R_d132527935e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">56</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0058_R_d132527935e841" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Nat. Commun.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, an enlarged interlayer between adjacent TM layers could lead to an unstable structure for Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation. With sufficient transport spacing for the Na<sup>+</sup> ions, the intermediate interlayer could be more conductive due to the interactions between TM layers and transported ions, thus improving the crystal stability and ionic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0031_R_d132527935e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">31</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0037_R_d132527935e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">37</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0059_R_d132527935e851" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J. Mater. Chem. A">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SLOs with a high entropy design and intermediate interlayer spacing could be a new path to further improve crystal stability and maintain optimal Na<sup>+</sup> transport and electronic conductivity.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we designed a high-entropy doped Cu, Fe, Mn-based layered oxide (HE-CFMO), Na<sub>0.95</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.20</sub>Fe<sub>0.22</sub>Mn<sub>0.35</sub>Ti<sub>0.13</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, with an O3 phase structure and moderated interlayer spacing (d = 0.54&nbsp;nm) as a cathode material for SIBs. Compared to the conventional Cu, Fe, Mn-based SLOs, Na<sub>0.95</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.20</sub>Fe<sub>0.35</sub>Mn<sub>0.40</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (CFMO, d = 0.55&nbsp;nm), the HE-CFMO provided significantly improved cycling stability and ion diffusion kinetics. The enhanced electrochemical performance of HE-CFMO is attributed to the cooperation of different doped elements with various ion radii that effectively stabilize the layered structure via an enhanced ion connection, offering a homogeneous stress distribution inside the crystal structure. Furthermore, the introduction of Li into the vacancies in the Na layers enhances the interactions between the adjacent TM layers, which improves the ionic conductivity and offers a pillar effect to buffer the volume change of the material during repeated Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation/deintercalation. The HE-CFMO cathodes exhibit high reversible capacity and remarkable cycling stability in both half-cells and full-cells, demonstrating their feasibility for practical applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410857-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410857-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The as-designed CFMO and HE-CFMO cathode materials were synthesized by solid-state reaction and formed an O3 phase according to neutron powder diffraction (NPD) refinement results (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410857-fig-0001">1a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) and X-ray diffraction (XRD) refinement results (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The pristine HE-CFMO cathode material has a typical layered oxide structure with alternating Na and TM layers. The Na occupies the 3a (0, 0, 0) sites in the octahedral sites, intercalating into the adjacent TM oxide layers, and parts of the Li penetrate the Na layers. The Li exists in both Na layers and TM layers with a ratio of 4:1, respectively. The vacancies of the Na layers allow Li to transfer inside the Na layers and form connections between the TM layers. The Li, Mg, Cu, Fe, Mn, and Ti randomly distribute at the 3<i>b</i> (0, 0, 0.5) sites to form high entropy TM layers that stabilize the entire structure of the HE-CFMO cathode material (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0001">1b</a>). The lattice parameters of HE-CFMO from the Rietveld refinement analysis of the neutron diffraction data are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.97647&nbsp;Å, <i>c</i> = 16.15543&nbsp;Å, and <i>V</i> = 123.952&nbsp;Å<sup>3</sup>, and the lattice parameters of CFMO are <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.96414&nbsp;Å, <i>c</i> = 16.33963&nbsp;Å, and <i>V</i> = 124.329&nbsp;Å<sup>3</sup>, respectively (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with CFMO, the HE-CFMO cathode material shows slightly narrower diffusion channels for Na<sup>+</sup> ions. This result is also confirmed by the XRD Rietveld refinement results (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The HE-CFMO and CFMO show slightly different lattice parameters with <i>c</i> = 16.16497&nbsp;Å for HE-CFMO and <i>c</i> = 16.35126&nbsp;Å for CFMO cathodes. The narrowed diffusion paths could be caused by the various TM elements with different ion radii fitting together and reinforced ion interactions between the adjacent TM layers due to the penetration of Li into the intercalated Na layer. The moderated interlayer spacing between the TM layers could provide a more close-knitted structure and prevent the drift of the metal ions inside the TM layers. Furthermore, the location of Li in HE-CFMO has been further demonstrated by the pair distribution function (PDF) refinement. The PDF data for both HE-CFMO (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) and CFMO (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) were refined with a high degree of agreement between experimental and calculated data, covering interatomic distances from 1.5 to 15.0&nbsp;Å. The refinement of HE-CFMO was performed based on the neutron diffraction-refined structure, which revealed partial Li ion occupancy in both the transition metal (TM) and Na layers. A low weighted profile R-factor (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub>) of 10.4% reflects a strong correlation between the experimental and simulated data, confirming the presence of Li ions within the Na layers. The refined unit cell parameters for HE-CFMO were determined to be <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.94288&nbsp;Å and <i>c</i> = 16.05100&nbsp;Å, while for CFMO, they were <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 2.93026&nbsp;Å and <i>c</i> = 16.19590&nbsp;Å. These findings indicate a slight contraction of the Na diffusion channels, likely due to the substitutional mixing of Li and Na within the Na layers. The Li ions occupy the vacancies in Na layers and form LiO<sub>6</sub> pillars, which could act as “springs” to provide a more flexible structure and buffer the potential structural distortion induced by repeatable Na (de)intercalation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0045_R_d132527935e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202410857-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/491f9d45-35ab-4513-8ae3-3fbce788db75/adma202410857-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/491f9d45-35ab-4513-8ae3-3fbce788db75/adma202410857-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/01a96f10-ca19-4f4d-94d7-97ae57e3a3d9/adma202410857-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/491f9d45-35ab-4513-8ae3-3fbce788db75/adma202410857-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410857-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410857" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rietveld refinement of the neutron diffraction pattern of HE-CFMO. b) Schematic of the crystal structure of O3 phase HE-CFMO. c) TEM image of HE-CFMO. d) HAADF-STEM of HE-CFMO. e) Fast Fourier transform (FFT) pattern extracted from (d). f) ICP Spectrometry result of HE-CFMO. g) TEM image with EDS mapping of HE-CFMO.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphology and microstructure of HE-CFMO and CFMO were investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The SEM images (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) and the TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) of HE-CFMO and CFMO show these two samples both have plate-like crystal shapes with a diameter of ≈2–5&nbsp;µm. High-angle annular dark-field TEM (HAADF-STEM) image of HE-CFMO shows a pure monocrystalline structure with clear lattice fringes and a lattice spacing of 0.255&nbsp;nm corresponding to the d-spacing of (1,0,1) crystal planes for the O3-type layered structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0001">1d</a>). A local fast Fourier transform (FFT) pattern was also extracted from the HAADF-STEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0001">1e</a>). The lattice distances corresponding to the d-spacing of the (1, 0, 1), (1, 1, 0), and (1, 0, 7) crystal planes of the O3-type layered structure measured from the FFT pattern further confirm the monocrystalline structure of the HE-CFMO.</p>
         
         <p>Inductively coupled plasma (ICP) spectrometry was used to study the contents of each element in both HE-CFMO and CFMO. The ICP result in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0001">1f</a> demonstrates the HE-CFMO has the format of Na<sub>0.95</sub>Li<sub>0.05</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.20</sub>Fe<sub>0.22</sub>Mn<sub>0.35</sub>Ti<sub>0.13</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The Na content with a mole fraction of 0.95 in HE-CFMO confirms the existence of vacancies in Na layers. As Li is dual-doped in both Na and TM layers with a ratio of 4:1 according to the NPD refinement result of HE-CFMO, the Li mole fractions in Na and TM sites are 0.04 and 0.01, respectively. The format of CFMO confirmed by ICP is Na<sub>0.95</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>Cu<sub>0.20</sub>Fe<sub>0.35</sub> Mn<sub>0.40</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The ICP spectrometry results confirm the TM layers of both HE-CFMO and CFMO samples are based on Cu, Fe, and Mn elements, and Mn ions dominate nearly 40% of the TM sites. In addition, the element distributions of HE-CFMO and CFMO were observed by energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mapping. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0001">1g</a> exhibits that Na, Mg, Ti, Cu, Fe, and Mn elements are homogeneously distributed in HE-CFMO. The CFMO comparison sample was also successfully prepared with the uniform distributions of Na, Mg, Cu, Fe, and Mn elements inside the cathode particles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The internal strain generated by the arrangement of various TM atoms has a sound impact on the lattice stability and structural integrity of the oxide materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0060_R_d132527935e1089" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nano Energy">60</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0061_R_d132527935e1089" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Small">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To estimate the internal strains at atomic levels, geometrical phase analysis (GPA) was performed for both HE-CFMO and CFMO cathode samples. The strain analysis is based on the Fourier transform of the aberration-corrected STEM images.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0062_R_d132527935e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Philos. Mag. A">62</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0063_R_d132527935e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Ultramicroscopy">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The atomic strain analysis of HE-CFMO and CFMO samples was processed based on the HAADF images (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410857-fig-0002">2a,b</a>). According to the strain analysis results, the tensile strain dominates the ɛ<sub>XX</sub> dimension in both HE-CFMO and CFMO samples. The strain distribution in the ɛ<sub>XX</sub> dimension of CFMO forms an in-homogeneous linear pattern, which indicates an uneven expanding stress affecting the lattice stability inside the CFMO sample. The inhomogeneous atomic strain distributions are even more aggressive in the ɛ<sub>XY</sub> and ɛ<sub>YY</sub> aspects, accompanied by uneven alternated tensile and compression strain linear patterns in CFMO. The abrupt strain field changes of CFMO indicate an unstable lattice structure, which could easily incur phase transition and lattice distortion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0064_R_d132527935e1115" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Nano Energy">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the tensile strain distribution of HE-CFMO is more uniform with a random distribution pattern in the ɛ<sub>XX</sub> dimension. In addition, homogeneous compression strain dominates in the ɛ<sub>XY</sub> and ɛ<sub>YY</sub> dimensions without obvious tensile strain, demonstrating a more uniform tensile and compression stress inside the high entropy designed lattice. Moreover, both the tensile strain and compression strain of HE-CFMO are significantly weaker than those of CFMO, which could avoid intragranular crack generation and suppress structural degradation during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0060_R_d132527935e1129" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nano Energy">60</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0061_R_d132527935e1129" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Small">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the GPA of HE-CFMO and CFMO cathode samples after 300 cycles at 1 C was also studied (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Although both cycled electrodes show more pronounced stains compared to the original samples, the HE-CFMO cathode exhibits weaker tensile and compression strains with a more random distribution than the CFMO cathode among ɛ<sub>XX</sub>, ɛ<sub>XY,</sub> and ɛ<sub>YY</sub> dimensions, demonstrating that the HE-CFMO cathode own stronger stress resilience and better structural integrity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202410857-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a99c063b-92e6-4c9d-bc8b-8d2956641ebf/adma202410857-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a99c063b-92e6-4c9d-bc8b-8d2956641ebf/adma202410857-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dca62df3-fd52-40ac-9f5a-45e3ab6e9d8b/adma202410857-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a99c063b-92e6-4c9d-bc8b-8d2956641ebf/adma202410857-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410857-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410857" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">HAADF images and the corresponding atomic strain distribution mapping of a) HE-CFMO and b) CFMO, where the red color in the analysis represents tensile strain, and the blue color represents compression strain. The ɛ<sub>XX</sub> represents the horizontal normal strain, ɛ<sub>XY</sub> represents the shear strain, and ɛ<sub>YY</sub> represents the vertical normal strain.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to analyze the elemental composition of HE-CFMO and CFMO. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), according to the binding energy of Li 1s, Mg 1s, Cu 2p, Fe 2p, and Ti 2p, the valence states of the corresponding elements in HE-CFMO are +1, +2, +2, +3, and +4, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0065_R_d132527935e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Catal., Struct. React.">65</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0070_R_d132527935e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - ACS Omega">70</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks of Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> can be identified as Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> with lower peak intensity of Mn<sup>4+</sup> than that of Mn<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0044_R_d132527935e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nanomicro Lett.">44</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0051_R_d132527935e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Stor. Mater.">51</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0071_R_d132527935e1194" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 71 - Am. Mineral.">71</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), the Mg 1s, Cu 2p, and Fe 2p peaks of CFMO show the same valence states of Mg, Cu and Fe elements in HE-CFMO. In addition, Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> also co-exist in CFMO. However, the peak intensity of Mn<sup>4+</sup> is higher than that of Mn<sup>3+</sup>. The XPS results indicate a higher content of Mn<sup>4+</sup> in CFMO compared to HE-CFMO. This difference could be caused by some Mn<sup>4+</sup> being substituted by the Ti<sup>4+</sup> in HE-CFMO. The substitution of Mn<sup>4+</sup> by Ti<sup>4+</sup> can inhibit charge ordering and promote electronic delocalization to reduce the TM−O interaction and increase the Na−O binding energy, leading to the expansion of TM layer spacing and the reduction of Na layer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0037_R_d132527935e1222" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">37</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0072_R_d132527935e1222" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 72 - Nat. Commun.">72</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result is in good agreement with the NPD and XRD results that demonstrate the moderate interlayer spacing of HE-CFMO, which is beneficial in increasing the ionic conductivity and crystal stability.</p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical performances of HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes were first evaluated by cyclic voltammogram (CV) measurements at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410857-fig-0003">3a,b</a>, compared with the CV curves of the CFMO cathode, the HE-CFMO cathode exhibits decreased anodic peak intensity at 2.5&nbsp;V, indicating the suppression of the Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction by the high entropy doping. This phenomenon provides a more stable crystal structure by preventing John–Teller effect due to the irreversible changes in Mn valence. Therefore, more capacity is contributed by the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> redox reactions at ≈3.4 and 3.7&nbsp;V, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0073_R_d132527935e1251" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 73 - Nat. Commun.">73</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the CV curves of the HE-CFMO cathode in the discharge process highly overlap from the first to the third cycles. In contrast, the CV curves of the CFMO cathode fluctuate due to the structural distortion induced by the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions during the previous charge process. Moreover, the charge/discharge curves of the cell with the HE-CFMO cathode further confirm the suppression of the 2.5&nbsp;V voltage plateau compared to the cell with the CFMO cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0003">3c</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202410857-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b06f159d-7118-4602-81d6-3c5124b0cd32/adma202410857-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b06f159d-7118-4602-81d6-3c5124b0cd32/adma202410857-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/93c5f67e-98b5-472a-adb7-a23fb966aee4/adma202410857-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b06f159d-7118-4602-81d6-3c5124b0cd32/adma202410857-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410857-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410857" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) CV curves of cells with (a) HE-CFMO cathode and (b) CFMO cathode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. c) Comparison of the first two cycle charge/discharge voltage profiles of the cells with HE-CFMO cathode and CFMO cathode at 0.5 C. d) The cycling performance of the cells with HE-CFMO cathode and CFMO cathode at 0.5 C. e) Long-term cycling performance of the cells with HE-CFMO cathode at 1 C. f) The rate performance of the cells with HE-CFMO cathode and CFMO cathode. g) Cycling performance of the full pouch cell with HE-CFMO cathode and HC anode (inset: illustration of the pouch cell lighting up the LED panel). h) The corresponding voltage profiles of the full cell at the 100th, 120th, and 150th cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The cycling performance of the HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes was evaluated at different current densities. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0003">3d</a> shows the HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes have comparable initial discharge capacities at ≈101.5 and 104.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C, respectively. However, the CFMO cathode only maintains ≈47% of the initial capacity (48.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) after 300 cycles. In contrast, the HE-CFMO cathode achieved excellent cycling stability with a capacity retention of 95% (96.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) after 300 cycles. After increasing the current density to 1 C, the HE-CFMO cathode also maintains a high-capacity retention of 83.7% after 500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0003">3e</a>). The rate performances of the as-prepared cathodes were tested at different current densities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0003">3f</a>). The discharge capacities of the HE-CFMO cathodes are 133.6, 121.0, 107.5, and 95.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 1.0 C, respectively, which are much higher than that of the CFMO cathode at the same current densities. The discharge capacity can be recovered to 123.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after the current density is reduced to 0.2 C, which demonstrates the high reversibility of the HE-CFMO cathodes. Moreover, the HE-CFMO cathodes prepared in this work achieved competitive electrochemical performance by applying non-toxic and cost-effective raw materials without Co and Ni elements (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To demonstrate the practical application of the as-designed material, we assembled pouch cells using HE-CFMO as the cathode and hard carbon (HC) as the anode. The charge/discharge performance of the HC anode is presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). A pouch cell with the HE-CFMO cathode and the HC anode was firstly charged/discharged for 2 cycles at 0.05 C, which obtained an initial capacity of 142.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (the capacity is calculated based on the mass of HE-CFMO active material only), and then the pouch cell was cycled at 0.5 C. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0003">3g</a>, the pouch cell achieved excellent cycling stability with a high reversible capacity of ≈105&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 150 cycles. Furthermore, an LED light panel was lit up by the pouch cell (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0003">3g</a>). Compared with the charge/discharge curves of the 100th, 120th, and 150th cycles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0003">3h</a>, the HE-CFMO pouch full cell delivered stable cycling performance after the initial capacity drop.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0051_R_d132527935e1330" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Stor. Mater.">51</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0074" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0074_R_d132527935e1330" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 74 - Nanoscale">74</a>-<a href="#adma202410857-bib-0081" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0081_R_d132527935e1330" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 81 - J. Mater. Chem. A">81</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In situ synchrotron XRD measurements were conducted to reveal the structural change of HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes during the charge/discharge processes. The XRD patterns in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410857-fig-0004">4a</a> show that the pristine HE-CFMO sample has an O3 phase before cycling. During the initial charge process, HE-CFMO gradually transferred from O3 phase to O3 + P3 phase and then fully converted to P3 phase when the electrode was charged to 4.0&nbsp;V. The P3 phase HE-CFMO has a larger interlayer space compared to the O3 phase HE-CFMO. During the subsequent discharge process, the HE-CFMO gradually transferred from P3 phase back to O3 + P3 phase until the electrode was discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V. The phase transfer process of CFMO during the first charge process is similar to HE-CFMO (O3 phase → O3 + P3 phase → P3 phase). However, after discharging the electrode to 2.0&nbsp;V, the CFMO sample still maintained P3 phase (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Interestingly, during the subsequent cycles, the HE-CFMO sample maintained the O3 + P3 phase, which is confirmed by the (003) peak of the XRD patterns in the 2nd and 20th discharge (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0004">4b</a>). In contrast, the CFMO sample is unable to revert to the O3 phase and instead maintains a P3 phase after discharge, this could be ascribed to the high kinetic barriers associated with the prismatic-to-octahedral site transition and John–Teller distortion derived from the presence of more Mn<sup>3+</sup> at 2.0&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0004">4b</a>). Furthermore, the interlayer expansion of HE-CFMO from discharge state to charge state (d<sub>difference</sub> = 0.0117&nbsp;nm) is ≈11% smaller than that in the CFMO sample (d<sub>difference</sub> = 0.0130&nbsp;nm). The reduced structural change of the HE-CFMO sample compared to the CFMO sample during cycling further confirms the benefit of high-entropy doping strategies on the cycling stability of Na-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202410857-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/744a674f-05c2-43a9-9907-300e294fa94d/adma202410857-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/744a674f-05c2-43a9-9907-300e294fa94d/adma202410857-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7e39fa6b-5ce7-467d-8924-f2ab69d651e5/adma202410857-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/744a674f-05c2-43a9-9907-300e294fa94d/adma202410857-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410857-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410857" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ synchrotron XRD pattern of a HE-CFMO cathode in the initial 1st and 2nd charge/discharge cycle. b) Change of the (003) peak of HE-CFMO in the (i) initial 1st and 2nd charge/discharge, and (ii) the 20th charge/discharge; the change of the (003) peak of CFMO in the (iii) initial 1st and 2nd charge/discharge, and (iv) the 20th charge/discharge. c) PEIS comparison between the SIBs after 20th cycles with the HE-CFMO cathode and CFMO cathode. d) Arrhenius plots of a HE-CFMO cathode at various voltages with various temperatures (<i>T</i> represents the absolute temperature), and the dashed lines indicate the linear fits of data points. e) Comparison of the activation energies at different voltages (2–4&nbsp;V) between the HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes. f) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity comparison between a HE-CFMO cathode (pristine and after 100 cycles) and a CFMO cathode (pristine and after 100 cycles).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further understand the effect of high-entropy design-induced moderated interlayer spacing on the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy was measured for SIBs with HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0004">4c</a> shows that the cell with HE-CFMO cathode offers a smaller charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of ≈416&nbsp;Ω compared with the CFMO cathode (≈718&nbsp;Ω), which reveals that the HE-CFMO with moderated interlayer spacing can offer an improved Na<sup>+</sup> transport behavior. In addition, the activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) was investigated to further study the electrochemical reaction kinetics of the HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of the HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes at various voltages and various temperatures were calculated from the EIS measurement data (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). The Arrhenius equation was used to obtain the <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> based on the obtained <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0082" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0082_R_d132527935e1417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 82 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">82</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the Arrhenius equation, <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2126c175-02f4-44eb-82c9-c530b6073aac/adma202410857-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410857:adma202410857-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410857-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>

                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\frac{{\ln ( {R_{{\mathrm{ct}}}^{ - 1}} )}}{{{{T}^{ - 1}}}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> in the equation, and the <i>R</i> is the universal gas constant. The rate of Na<sup>+</sup> ion desolvation process is described by the reciprocal of charge transfer resistance. The calculated <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> results are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0004">4e</a>, where the error bars indicate the standard deviation derived from two tests for three independent cathodes, and the data are presented as mean values ± standard deviation. The <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> results indicate that the HE-CFMO cathode requires lower activation energy (≈34–52&nbsp;kJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>) than the CFMO cathode (≈45–76&nbsp;kJ&nbsp;mol<sup>−1</sup>) for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion during charging/discharging.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the improved Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements were conducted on both HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients were calculated based on the GITT results at different charge and discharge stages in the 1st and 100th cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0004">4f</a>). The HE-CFMO cathode provides a Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of ≈2.4&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup> along the plateau corresponding to the O3 phase and 6.5&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> in the slope region corresponding to the O3+P3 phase during the initial charge process. In contrast, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of the CFMO cathode are much lower than the HE-CFMO cathode at the same charge stages (≈1.7&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup> at the plateau and 4.9&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> in the slope region, respectively), indicating that the HE-CFMO cathode has enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics. After 100 cycles, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of the HE-CFMO cathode are barely changed and are still much higher than those of the CFMO cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0004">4f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). According to the calculation, the average D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of the HE-CFMO cathode after 100 cycles (1.1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>) is almost five times higher than that of the CFMO cathode after 100 cycles (2.1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>). The improved reaction kinetic is further supported by the comparison of the cyclic voltammetry tests at different scan rates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). The comparisons of the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>), and diffusion coefficients between the HE-CFMO and CFMO demonstrate that the moderated interlayer spacing in the high entropy design can provide a stable host structure without restricting Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>To study the charge compensation mechanism of the HE-CFMO cathodes, XPS analysis was used to identify the value change of the elements that participate in the redox reactions and contribute to the capacity during charging/discharging (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). Initially, the Cu, Fe, and Mn elements in HE-CFMO are divalent, trivalent, and trivalent/tetravalent, respectively. When the electrodes were charged to 4&nbsp;V, the binding energy of Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub> shifted from 933.2 to 933.8&nbsp;eV due to parts of Cu<sup>2+</sup> oxidized to Cu<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0039_R_d132527935e1593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Energy Chem.">39</a>, <a href="#adma202410857-bib-0083" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0083_R_d132527935e1593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 83 - J. Mater. Chem. A">83</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Fe 2p3/2 binding energy also slightly shifted from 710.5 to 710.8&nbsp;eV due to the oxidation of Fe<sup>3+</sup> to Fe<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0084" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0084_R_d132527935e1603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 84 - J. Energy Chem.">84</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the binding energy of Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> drifted from 641.9 to 642.6&nbsp;eV due to the conversion of Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0051_R_d132527935e1615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Stor. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After being discharged to 2&nbsp;V, the binding energy of Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> reduced to 641.2&nbsp;eV due to the reappearance of the Mn<sup>3+</sup>. The Mg and Ti maintained no valence change throughout the charge/discharge process. The XPS results indicate the Cu, Fe, and Mn elements are all contributing to the electric capacity of the HE-CFMO cathode, and the Mg and Ti act as rivets to prevent the ion drift and phase change, thus stabilizing the TM layers and contributing to the enhanced cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>The charge compensation in the bulk of the HE-CFMO and CFMO cathode materials was further studied by X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS). According to the previous XPS results, there are no valence changes for Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> in the selected charge/discharge voltage range. Hence, only the Mn, Fe, and Cu elements were characterized by XAS. The position of the K-edge for HE-CFMO in the original state was compared with reference materials (Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, MnO<sub>2</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, and CuO), which confirmed the valence state of Mn, Fe, and Cu are trivalent/tetravalent, trivalent and divalent, which are all agreed with XPS results (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). The normalized X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra of Mn K-edge, Fe K-edge, and Cu K-edge of HE-CFMO and CFMO are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410857-fig-0005">5a–f</a>. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0005">5a</a>, when charged to 4&nbsp;V, the Mn K-edge XANES spectra shifted to higher energy, indicating the redox process from Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup>. After being discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, the Mn K-edge XANES spectra shifted to slightly lower energy than the original value, indicating parts of Mn<sup>4+</sup> reversibly reduced to Mn<sup>3+</sup>. In contrast, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0005">5d</a> indicates the Mn K-edge XANES spectra of the CFMO cathode shifted to a higher energy when charged to 4.0&nbsp;V, and then shifted to a significantly lower energy than the original value, which indicates more Mn<sup>4+</sup> reduced to Mn<sup>3+</sup> in the CFMO cathode than in HE-CFMO cathode, which could cause a serious John–Teller distortion in CFMO. This difference might be due to the doping effect of Li<sup>+</sup> ions in TM layers, which increases and fixes the valence of surrounding Mn in a Mn<sup>4+</sup> state, thus providing an enhanced stable structure.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202410857-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/57a73a43-a901-4409-8259-1a9d45202b05/adma202410857-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/57a73a43-a901-4409-8259-1a9d45202b05/adma202410857-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d86b0666-598c-47bf-824a-fb99b3db3e1d/adma202410857-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/57a73a43-a901-4409-8259-1a9d45202b05/adma202410857-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410857-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410857" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The normalized ex situ XANES spectra of a) Mn K-edge, b) Fe K-edge, and c) Cu K-edge for a HE-CFMO cathode in pristine, charged, and discharged states. The normalized ex situ XANES spectra of d) Mn K-edge, e) Fe K-edge, and f) Cu K-edge for a CFMO cathode in pristine, charged, and discharged states. The corresponding EXAFS spectra of g) Mn K-edge, h) Fe K-edge, and i) Cu K-edge for a HE-CFMO cathode in pristine, charged, and discharged states. The corresponding EXAFS spectra of j) Mn K-edge, k) Fe K-edge, and l) Cu K-edge for a CFMO cathode in pristine, charged, and discharged states.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Similarly, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0005">5b</a> exhibits that when HE-CFMO cathodes were charged to 4.0&nbsp;V, the Fe K-edge XANES spectra slightly shifted to a higher energy, corresponding to the oxidization of Fe<sup>3+</sup> to a higher valence. After discharging the cathode to 2.0&nbsp;V, the Fe K-edge XANES spectra returned to the original value, indicating a highly reversible redox reaction of the Fe element. Furthermore, the Cu K-edge XANES spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0005">5c</a> also exhibited a slight shift to higher energy when charged to 4.0&nbsp;V with part of the Cu<sup>2+</sup> oxidizing to Cu<sup>3+</sup>, and then returning to the original condition after being discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V. The Fe and Cu K-edge XANES spectra of the CFMO cathode show similar charge compensation mechanisms to the HE-CFMO cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0005">5e,f</a>). However, the Fe K-edge and Cu K-edge of CFMO cathode did not return to the original location when cathode samples were discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, which might be due to irreversible structural change. The comparison of XANES spectra between the HE-CFMO and CFMO cathodes further demonstrates the improved stability of the layered structure in the high-entropy doped HE-CFMO material. Furthermore, the corresponding extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra of the Mn K-edges, Fe K-edges, and Cu K-edges for HE-CFMO and CFMO are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410857-fig-0005">5g–l</a>, respectively. The two major peaks in the K-edges of the EXAFS spectra represent the average TM─O and TM─TM length.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0085" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0085_R_d132527935e1719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 85 - Energy Stor. Mater.">85</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The deviations of Fourier transform (FT) magnitude for both TM─O and TM─TM in all the K-edge spectra of the HE-CFMO cathode are smaller than those of the CFMO cathodes, demonstrating a higher structure stability of the HE-CFMO cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410857-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410857-bib-0046_R_d132527935e1724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the increase/decrease of Mn─O, Fe─O, and Cu─O bond lengths in HE-CFMO cathodes exhibit significantly higher reversibility than that of the CFMO cathodes during the charge and discharge processes, implying the effectiveness of the high entropy doping design on the structure stability of the TM layers in the layered oxides.</p>
         
         <p>The charge compensation mechanisms and structural changes of HE-CFMO and CFMO cathode materials during charging/discharging are summarized in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410857-fig-0006">6</a>. In the pristine state of HE-CFMO, the Li<sup>+</sup> ions are dual-doped in Na and TM sites that connect the adjacent TM layers and fix the surrounding Mn<sup>4+</sup> valence state. The Cu, Fe, and Mn components are the main redox species to provide Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage capacity. The high entropy doping designed TM layers offer a stable and high conductive moderated interlayered spacing as fast ion transport channels to accommodate Na<sup>+</sup> ions. When a HE-CFMO electrode is charged to 4.0&nbsp;V, Na<sup>+</sup> ions deintercalate from the interlayers, accompanied by the oxidation of Mn/Cu/Fe components and the expansion of the interlayer spacing. Fortunately, the doping of Li<sup>+</sup> in the TM layer increases the Mn valence and reduces John–Teller effect when the HE-CFMO electrode is discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V. Meanwhile, Li<sup>+</sup> doping into vacancies in the Na layer acts as “springs” to span the adjacent TM layers and maintain the moderated interlayer spacing, while Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> ions act as “rivets” with unchanged valence to immobilize the layered structure during the charge/discharge process. Moreover, a summary of the contributions of each element in HE-CFMO is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). In contrast, when a CFMO cathode is charged to 4.0&nbsp;V, the interlayer spacing expansion occurs along with the oxidation of Mn, Cu, and Fe components. However, after the CFMO cathode is discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, the change of Mn valence leads to severe John–Teller distortion, causing the collapse of the layered structure. The structural distortion may impede Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion and reduce Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage, thus leading to significant capacity fading. Furthermore, the unrecovered volume expansion further reduces the ionic conductivity, aggravates the structure distortion in the following cycling, and even leads to the dissolution of the cathode material components into the electrolyte. Thus, the HE-CFMO cathode material with the high-entropy TM layers and Li-doped Na layers provides relatively unobstructed and conductive ion transport channels and maintains the intact layered structure during the Na<sup>+</sup> ion (de)intercalation. Therefore, the HE-CFMO provides a highly reversible capacity and super cycling performance.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202410857-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aa620c3a-31a7-4004-a086-404aa7369dff/adma202410857-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/aa620c3a-31a7-4004-a086-404aa7369dff/adma202410857-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7f2b4843-7d66-4d15-8016-f029495857d5/adma202410857-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aa620c3a-31a7-4004-a086-404aa7369dff/adma202410857-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410857-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410857" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic of the charge compensation and structure change mechanism throughout the change/discharge of a) HE-CFMO and b) CFMO (the sizes of element balls are according to the valence states following the rule of higher valence having smaller size).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410857-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410857-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we designed and prepared a high entropy doped O3 phase Cu, Fe, Mn-based sodium layered oxide (HE-CFMO) cathode material by incorporating Li, Mg, and Ti elements with synergistic functionalities for enhanced performance for SIBs. Specifically, the high entropy design at TM layers leads to homogeneous stress distribution and robust close-knitted structure frames which suppress intragranular crack generation and structural degradation during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation. Additionally, Mg and Ti dopants in TM layers act as fixed ‘rivets’ to prevent ion drift and structural distortion, while Li doping increases the Mn valence state, preventing John–Teller effect and stabilizing the layered structure. Thanks to doped Li partially substituting Na in the Na layers, strengthening the connection between adjacent TM layers and stabilizing the Na diffusion channels, this resulted from the moderated interlayer spacing of HE-CFMO offers high Na ion conductivity and low activation energy, as demonstrated by the EIS, activation energy, and GITT testing results. Therefore, HE-CFMO cathodes provide significantly improved electrochemical performance compared to conventional CFMO electrodes in Na-ion batteries, presenting a promising strategy to further develop low-cost and eco-friendly sodium layer oxide cathode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410857-sec-0040-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
         
         <p>Z.H. and S.W. contributed equally to this work. G.X.W. would like to thank the financial support from the Australian Research Council (ARC) through the ARC Discovery Project (DP240102176), and ARC Research Hub for Integrated Energy Storage Solutions (IH180100020). B.S. would like to thank the financial support from ARC through the ARC Future Fellowship (FT220100561). The authors thank the support on synchrotron and neutron characterizations from Powder Diffraction Beamline (Granted No. M18798) of Australia Synchrotron, Australian Centre for Neutron Scattering, National Synchrotron Radiation Research Centre, and I15-1 Beamline (Granted No. CY36852) of Diamond Light Source. The authors also appreciate the support of transition electron microscopy imaging by Dr. Jiangtao Qu at the Australian Centre for Microscopy &amp; Microanalysis.</p>
